        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3276810346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16542784743565576378">This book is magical. Sometimes the writing is challenging, and a little frustrating, but it’s lyrical, gorgeous, and devastating.<br/><br/>An initial reference point for me was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/176372.Madeline_Miller' title='Madeline Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Madeline Miller</a>’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11250317.The_Song_of_Achilles' title='The Song of Achilles by Madeline Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Song of Achilles</a> - which I suspect owes much to this novel and its predecessor in the Alexander trilogy, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67697.Fire_from_Heaven__Alexander_the_Great___1_' title='Fire from Heaven (Alexander the Great, #1) by Mary Renault' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fire from Heaven</a>. It deals with almost the exact same theme of adolescent hero-worship and erotic love between boys in a quasi-mythical setting. In Miller, it’s the mythical hero Achilles and his BFF/lover</span>
  <span id="freeText16542784743565576378" style="display:none">This book is magical. Sometimes the writing is challenging, and a little frustrating, but it’s lyrical, gorgeous, and devastating.<br /><br />An initial reference point for me was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/176372.Madeline_Miller" title="Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Madeline Miller</a>’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11250317.The_Song_of_Achilles" title="The Song of Achilles by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song of Achilles</a> - which I suspect owes much to this novel and its predecessor in the Alexander trilogy, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67697.Fire_from_Heaven__Alexander_the_Great___1_" title="Fire from Heaven (Alexander the Great, #1) by Mary Renault" rel="nofollow noopener">Fire from Heaven</a>. It deals with almost the exact same theme of adolescent hero-worship and erotic love between boys in a quasi-mythical setting. In Miller, it’s the mythical hero Achilles and his BFF/lover Patroclus. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67697.Fire_from_Heaven__Alexander_the_Great___1_" title="Fire from Heaven (Alexander the Great, #1) by Mary Renault" rel="nofollow noopener">Fire from Heaven</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/67700.The_Persian_Boy__Alexander_the_Great___2_" title="The Persian Boy (Alexander the Great, #2) by Mary Renault" rel="nofollow noopener">The Persian Boy</a>, it’s a similar relationship between Alexander the Great and one of his generals Hephaestion.<br /><br />The difference here is that there’s a third wheel, who narrates this novel, the eponymous boy, Bagoas. He’s not exactly a rival for Alexander’s affection so much as another worshipper at Alexander’s altar. He’s a eunuch and royal concubine, and he describes his story with a sense of classical inevitability until he reaches his “purpose” in life - to love and serve Alexander.<br /><br />It’s beautifully written and you need to read it really slowly because every sentence is packed with meaning. Take your time, have a coffee or a glass of wine, put your feet up and be transported back into a very different, brutal, subtle, but powerfully emotive world.<br /><br />Read the New Yorker article linked below for a personal &amp; informative essay on this book and other Renault works. It situates her writing in the time she wrote, including her success (interestingly as a lesbian) at writing gay male characters, often but not always set in the classical world.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2013/01/07/the-american-boy">https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/20...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16542784743565576378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer382076395" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5717093990328392369">Mary Renault is a master historical novelist. I love the way she showed us Alexander the Great differently than he is often portrayed in the history books and Pressfield novels. I enjoy Pressfield's work as well but to him Alexander was first and foremost a classical Hellenist and warrior. Renault focuses on Alexander the lover how he was viewed by his Persian boy; a eunuch, a slave, but also an influential master of his privy chamber. I was bothered in the beginning by Renault's romanticizing w</span>
  <span id="freeText5717093990328392369" style="display:none">Mary Renault is a master historical novelist. I love the way she showed us Alexander the Great differently than he is often portrayed in the history books and Pressfield novels. I enjoy Pressfield's work as well but to him Alexander was first and foremost a classical Hellenist and warrior. Renault focuses on Alexander the lover how he was viewed by his Persian boy; a eunuch, a slave, but also an influential master of his privy chamber. I was bothered in the beginning by Renault's romanticizing what would be consider modern pedophilia but then I think it is important for historical novels to accurately depict the times without whitewash. Bogoas is the focus of the novel and she remains true to his viewpoint. The book did seem to downplay Alexander's wife Roxanne, and his mother Olympias, in the story and that was unfortunate. But then this is the third Renault novel I have read and women rarely play powerful roles. She also gives short shift to his generals, Ptolemy and Hephastion. She is an excellent writer and this is considered one of her most influential works. In my mind it fell slightly short of "Funeral Games".</span>
  <a data-text-id="5717093990328392369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer129095682" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8448940731987946847">Some favorite parts:<br/><br/>"We had our summer. On the hills with the hounds crying, racing the clouds; in the rose garden with its lotus pools; in the high hall whose columns were sheathed with gold and silver, as I did my Dance of the River to the sound of flutes; in the great Bedchamber where I had been shamed and now was cherished, each day and night, I used to say to myself, I will miss nothing; I will never let my eye or my ear or my soul or my senses sleep, never forget to know that I am happy. F</span>
  <span id="freeText8448940731987946847" style="display:none">Some favorite parts:<br /><br />"We had our summer. On the hills with the hounds crying, racing the clouds; in the rose garden with its lotus pools; in the high hall whose columns were sheathed with gold and silver, as I did my Dance of the River to the sound of flutes; in the great Bedchamber where I had been shamed and now was cherished, each day and night, I used to say to myself, I will miss nothing; I will never let my eye or my ear or my soul or my senses sleep, never forget to know that I am happy. For it will be a long campaign; and who knows when we shall come back?<br /><br />Thus the Wise God gives prophecy enough, but not too much; as he does to birds, who foresee the winter, but not the night of ice that will drop them from the bough.<br /><br />...<br /><br />Never let a moment flow by me unthanked, unkissed." -pg 376<br /><br />"...the prophetic voice was silent, which had said at Ekbatana, 'Carve this upon your heart.' It spared me; it did not say, 'Never again.'" -pg 406<br /></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1200744-aldi?shelf=historical">historical</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2540065812" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10739775969191418290">Basically flawless. They don't write them like this anymore.<br/>Normally when I really love a book, I tear through ravenously, but this one made me linger. I'd often find myself meandering, revisiting an earlier scene in a new light, savouring a paragraph I'd just read, occasionally going all the way back to the beginning or browsing forward so I'd know when to steel myself for heartache (yeah, that one didn't work). It's well worth lingering over. Renault's style is at its best here, subtle, lyrica</span>
  <span id="freeText10739775969191418290" style="display:none">Basically flawless. They don't write them like this anymore.<br />Normally when I really love a book, I tear through ravenously, but this one made me linger. I'd often find myself meandering, revisiting an earlier scene in a new light, savouring a paragraph I'd just read, occasionally going all the way back to the beginning or browsing forward so I'd know when to steel myself for heartache (yeah, that one didn't work). It's well worth lingering over. Renault's style is at its best here, subtle, lyrical, hauntingly lovely. (It makes me sad because there's so much stupid prescriptive writing advice being handed out to young writers these days telling them to avoid exposition altogether, when there are books like this that show how perfectly an exposition-heavy style can work when well done.) I loved Bagoas's voice, and the beautiful details of the character insights. I'd braced myself for Hephaistion getting side-lined, but he wasn't as much as I'd expected; if anything, there was so much between the lines, and in Bagoas's grudging concessions about Hephaistion's character as time went by, that made me ache for both of them, and wish they could have been friends. And the last 100 or so pages just kept breaking me into ever more shattered pieces, in the best way. Definitely one for the "all-time favourites" pile.</span>

<div id="review_737157276" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/737157276" />
    <a title="Crystal Starr Light" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3990525-crystal-starr-light"><img alt="Crystal Starr Light" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573066911p2/3990525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/737157276?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crystal Starr Light" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crystal Starr Light" href="/user/show/3990525-crystal-starr-light">Crystal Starr Light</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Crystal Starr Light by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4973578-iset">Iset</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3990525-crystal-starr-light?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3990525-crystal-starr-light?shelf=gift">gift</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3990525-crystal-starr-light?shelf=2014-owned-challenge">2014-owned-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer737157276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer955540540555530135">Bullet Review:<br/><br/>Wow.<br/><br/>That ending!! Where's the next book?! How can it just end like that?!<br/><br/>Full Review:<br/><br/>Alexander the Great (or, according to my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4973578-isis' rel='nofollow noopener'>personal historian</a>, as his title should be known, in its native tongue, "o Megas Alexandros") is absolutely an awe-inspiring person. When you put into perspective the time period, the cultures, and then think about the things Alexander did, it's absolutely extraordinary.<br/><br/>This is the story of Alexander's later life, as told through the eyes of his Persian "boy</span>
  <span id="freeText955540540555530135" style="display:none">Bullet Review:<br /><br />Wow.<br /><br />That ending!! Where's the next book?! How can it just end like that?!<br /><br />Full Review:<br /><br />Alexander the Great (or, according to my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/4973578-isis" rel="nofollow noopener">personal historian</a>, as his title should be known, in its native tongue, "o Megas Alexandros") is absolutely an awe-inspiring person. When you put into perspective the time period, the cultures, and then think about the things Alexander did, it's absolutely extraordinary.<br /><br />This is the story of Alexander's later life, as told through the eyes of his Persian "boy", Bagoas, who also (in this story and perhaps in real life) was his lover. Through Bagoas' eyes, we see Alexander's extraordinary feats, but we also see how human Alexander is - how he was flawed, how he tired, how he loved, how he raged.<br /><br />Last year, I started "Fire from Heaven", the first book in the Alexander trilogy, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/737156964" rel="nofollow noopener">my thoughts</a> were all over the place. Mostly, I recall the writing being a challenge to get through (probably because of all the crap young adult I had been reading up to that time where subtlety and complexity are taboo). But I did not find this the case at all with "The Persian Boy". I personally loved the first person past tense used here - even more so because Renault executes it PERFECTLY!<br /><br />First person is very chic these days, and it can be used well. It's plopping right into the head of your narrator and seeing and feeling and experiencing everything he or she does. That can be awesome - but it can also be limiting, ESPECIALLY for historical novels, or novels where something important happens away from the narrator.<br /><br />"Eclipse" combated that with a cheesy "Bella is asleep but in her dreams hears Edward and Jacob talk about her" scene. Other novels have characters butt themselves in where they don't belong, miraculously receive information that is just what the reader needs, a chain of messengers, have the narrator skip over that time period and summarize it, or the absolute worst, switch to third person past to relay the scene.<br /><br />Renault never does that; Bagoas is a pretty prominent character, part of Alexander's court, so it makes sense he would know some of what he does. In the beginning, Bagoas is a lesser eunuch in Darius' court and therefore knows almost nothing about what is going on, unless he is busy asking others questions. I'm not really doing a great job of saying it, but what I mean is: Renault doesn't feel the need to mangle her story so that Bagoas can relay everything to the audience.<br /><br />I would say most of the characters are very relatable and, most importantly, REAL. Bagoas had his moments where he annoyed me (I think part of him was just in love with being in love with someone), but overall, his story was interesting, compelling, heart-rending, and passionate. I loved how he didn't try to supersede Alexander - such as giving Alexander key advice how to win battles and crap - nor does he also do the other thing I hate in first person: make all the characters he doesn't like evil, wicked people.<br /><br />Bagoas doesn't like Hephaistion because he wants to be the only one to love Alexander. (By the way, this is the part that aggravated me most about Bagoas, because I LOVED the way Renault wrote Hephaistion and Alexander - if we are going to do that silly "team" thing that got popularized in "Twilight", I am Team Hephaistion.) But at no point does it mean that Hephaistion is an evil guy. Nope, it is clearly Bagoas' opinion. And most authors wouldn't do that. When I read Phillipa Gregory's "The Other Boleyn Girl", it was clear that not only did Mary think Anne was evil (or Jane a busybody), but the author thought that Anne was evil, and Anne was, therefore, written as an evil woman.<br /><br />There is a HUGE amount of STUFFS that happen in this book, making it a really long book. But unlike many really long books, it's not like there's a scene or a section where I can say, "The Author should have red-inked that". In fact, if anything, I think the author could have put MORE into it. For instance, Bagoas hints that he lives in Egypt with Ptolemy at the end - how does that happen? Did he create a relationship with Ptolemy? If so, I would have loved to see the conversations.<br /><br />This really is an incredible book looking at the life of Alexander. For a moment, I got to see how MUCH he accomplished, and yet how much more he wanted to do. Renault's Alexander comes alive on the pages through Bagoas' eyes, showing him to be a vivacious, intelligent, larger-than-life man - and yet just a man still.<br /><br />This is a pretty meaty book, and I found it better to enjoy in larger chunks than a page or two at a time. This, coupled with my occasional aggravation with Bagoas and my desire to leave that final star open for the final book in the series, is what causes this to be rated 4 stars instead of the full 5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="955540540555530135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_544406896" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/544406896" />
    <a title="Alicja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4745220-alicja"><img alt="Alicja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411681297p2/4745220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544406896?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alicja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alicja" href="/user/show/4745220-alicja">Alicja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=ancient-greece">ancient-greece</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=pre-gun-battles">pre-gun-battles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=ancient-world">ancient-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=war-military-battle">war-military-battle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=alexander-the-great">alexander-the-great</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=dream-library">dream-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544406896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13721027698739604899">rating: 6/5!<br/><br/>I am in love with Bagoas' Alexander!<br/><br/>This second (because it wouldn’t be like me to actually read books in their intended order, now would it?) in Renault’s trilogy about the life of Alexander the Great. This novel is told from the point of view of Bagoas, his lover and servant. Bagoas, Persian king Darius’ eunuch slave, was given to Alexander by a Persian noble to gain favor after they murdered Darius. Alexander sets him free but Bagoas chooses to stay and serve him, and then proceed</span>
  <span id="freeText13721027698739604899" style="display:none">rating: 6/5!<br /><br />I am in love with Bagoas' Alexander!<br /><br />This second (because it wouldn’t be like me to actually read books in their intended order, now would it?) in Renault’s trilogy about the life of Alexander the Great. This novel is told from the point of view of Bagoas, his lover and servant. Bagoas, Persian king Darius’ eunuch slave, was given to Alexander by a Persian noble to gain favor after they murdered Darius. Alexander sets him free but Bagoas chooses to stay and serve him, and then proceeds to fall in love with and seduce the king. Through his eyes we witness epic battles, treachery within the king’s ranks, political intrigue, injuries and sickness, drama with Alexander’s wives, rivalry with Hephaesiton for Alexander’s affection, etc. until the king’s mysterious death at the age of 32, just a month short of his 33rd birthday.<br /><br />Bagoas lost his family as a child to traitors and was sold into slavery after being castrated to retain his youthful and beautiful looks. He had been used by a few Persian households until he was bought to be trained to serve king Darius as one of his (hundreds? thousands? male and female) concubines. Alexander (and his father, Philip) at this point had already united the Greeks and had been crowned the Pharaoh of Egypt. Already a legend with an almost god-like reputation (or one of Zeus’ son as proclaimed by the oracle of Delphi), he set out to conquer Persia.<br /><br />When Alexander received Bagoas as a gift, he let him stay as a servant if he wished or be free. It wasn’t until Bagoas decided to seduce the king that the story took on mythic propitiations. Alexander, through Bagoas’ narration, is presented as very human and flawed, a man who deeply craves love from everyone. Bagoas wasn’t blind to his murders, ruthlessness in war, short temper, drunkenness, etc. He not only saw Alexander at his most vulnerable, as a man who loved and hurt and grieved, but also as a man who was larger than life, one who dreamt big and was fearless on the battlefield. It is such a unique look at Alexander’s life that you can’t help but fall in love with the king. Bagoas wasn’t just a passive observer either. He loved Alexander but had his own insecurities. He questioned his status in Alexander’s court, being seen as a Persian “barbarian” among, in his view, the “barbaric” Macedonians. He also questioned his place with Alexander among the king’s wives and Hephaestion, Alexander’s lifelong best friend and lover.<br /><br />Throughout, we see a version of Alexander that historical books, sparse on the details of his private life, can’t provide. The author invented most of the details regarding Bagoas because he is only mentioned a few times in historical texts (the fact that a eunuch Persian slave boy was mentioned at all in relation to Alexander would seem to signify that he has had a significant role). The narrative does an amazing job describing the sights and sounds of the ancient world marred in war and ethnic conflicts. This novel is emotional, sorrowful and joyful, and will lead the reader from tears to laughter and back to tears again during the tragic and mysterious ending of the king’s life. Renault may lay it on a bit thick at the end describing the omens of impending death but when taken into context, the Greeks did pay much attention to symbols and prophecies, in turn the descriptions provide yet another layer of depth. <br /><br />It is a marvelous, completely brilliant book based on all the facts available regarding Alexander’s life and filled in beautifully with fiction by Mary Renault. Her words are almost poetic and draw you in from the start. She weaves history, realism, myth, and invention in such a way that it leads you to believe that these really were the details of Alexander’s life, as written by Bagoas after the king’s death (and the first person point of view is literary magic in her hands). This is truly a must read novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13721027698739604899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_943646167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/943646167" />
    <a title="Seth Reeves" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31572845-seth-reeves"><img alt="Seth Reeves" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400650705p2/31572845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/943646167?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth Reeves" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth Reeves" href="/user/show/31572845-seth-reeves">Seth Reeves</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18522477-the-persian-boy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer943646167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1731999590188242687"><b>2.5 Stars</b><br/><br/>Can't say I hated this book nor that I liked it, really. The story is narrated entirely by a eunuch, Bagoas, that Alexander is gifted by a defeated Persian lord early on in Alexander's conquest of Persia and Asia in general. The eunuch loves Alexander so much that it gets annoying as the story goes on. He is constantly going on about his love for Alexander and their nights together, also in very 1950s prudish expansive prose. I'm not opposed to reading about relationships but as this is</span>
  <span id="freeText1731999590188242687" style="display:none"><b>2.5 Stars</b><br /><br />Can't say I hated this book nor that I liked it, really. The story is narrated entirely by a eunuch, Bagoas, that Alexander is gifted by a defeated Persian lord early on in Alexander's conquest of Persia and Asia in general. The eunuch loves Alexander so much that it gets annoying as the story goes on. He is constantly going on about his love for Alexander and their nights together, also in very 1950s prudish expansive prose. I'm not opposed to reading about relationships but as this is a telling of the story of a great military leader I would have enjoyed the narrative more if we could have heard from some of the soldiers, sailors or other conquered peoples regarding the events that took place.<br /><br />The book is mind-numbingly repetitive in several aspects. There's a line in one of the later chapters where Bagoas says "Alexander inspired jealously" and I just wanted to scream "No shit, Sherlock! You've only been saying how fucking jealous everyone one is of him and his favor for the past 400 pages!" Even the war scenarios took on this repetitiveness after a while. He keeps explaining in different ways that Alexander rewards fealty and punishes betrayal. After this happens for the sixth or seventh time I think I got the point.<br /><br />I guess my biggest criticism of the book is that it's about an extremely interesting historical figure and the book is just barely readable. It's amazing how a really great writer can stoke your fascination in the mundane or the unknown while a middling writer takes on a well known and intriguing subject and actually saps away your interest, as is the case with this book.<br /><br />In my heart I am a lover of history and this was a not completely unpleasant way to deepen my knowledge about the life and death of Alexander the Great. <br />I only hope that another better storyteller takes a swing at this source material and manages to wring out of it a better novel for future generations to learn about and immerse themselves in one of the western world's first well-chronicled major historical figures.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1731999590188242687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2801670277" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2801670277" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74992809-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513339232p2/74992809.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2801670277?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/74992809-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2801670277" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13083213601082185410">After finishing <i>Fire From Heaven</i>, Mary Renault could have easily given us more of the same. She instead opts for something different, and it's clearly the right choice.<br/><br/><i>The Persian Boy</i> is narrated by Bagoas, the son of a noble house who - following betrayal and treachery - is sold into slavery. After the Persians' defeat at Gaugamela, Bagoas is given to Alexander as tribute. This allows Renault to depict her hero from the Persians' point-of-view: his spartan lifestyle and lack of pretense strike </span>
  <span id="freeText13083213601082185410" style="display:none">After finishing <i>Fire From Heaven</i>, Mary Renault could have easily given us more of the same. She instead opts for something different, and it's clearly the right choice.<br /><br /><i>The Persian Boy</i> is narrated by Bagoas, the son of a noble house who - following betrayal and treachery - is sold into slavery. After the Persians' defeat at Gaugamela, Bagoas is given to Alexander as tribute. This allows Renault to depict her hero from the Persians' point-of-view: his spartan lifestyle and lack of pretense strike Bagoas as really uncouth, and Bagoas wonders more than once what sort of people he's living among...<br /> <br />At the same time, Renault traces the developing bond between them, as Bagoas (like Hephaistion before him) falls deeply in love with Alexander. Bagoas soon becomes his constant companion and gives us a first-hand account of the last seven years of Alexander's life: his marriage to Roxane; his invasion of India; the long, slow march back to Babylon; his incredible grief following Hephaistion's death.<br /><br />Renault, of course, lays the groundwork for all of this in the first book, where the maxim "Nothing can be had for nothing" is repeated several times, and where it's remarked that when men will no longer follow Alexander, he will surely begin to die. This foreshadowing doesn't tip over into fatalism, though: in <i>The Persian Boy's</i> most telling scene, Alexander remarks that "one must live as if it would be forever, and as if one might die each moment. Always both at once..."<br /><br />If I had to sum up Renault's Alexander novels in a single line, that'd be it. Because <i>Fire From Heaven</i> and <i>The Persian Boy</i> are ultimately more than historical fiction or gay coming-of-age stories. As Daniel Mendelsohn <a href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2013/01/07/the-american-boy" rel="nofollow noopener">pointed out</a>, Renault looks to the ancient past in order to help us envision a different future: a future in which m/m relationships are neither camp nor tragic but are just as genuine and just as heartfelt as any other.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13083213601082185410" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18427457235308105852">Part of me really disliked this book; part of me couldn't help but like it. The language gets a little flowery sometimes, esp when she starts talking about love and (always cleverly elided) sex. The close adherence to history made parts of it read less-than-exciting. (i.e. "Great, now Alexander is going to preside over more games.... when is he going to drop dead alreadygjkfgjkdfg?!") I like Alexander but clearly not as much as the deadly combination of Renault-through-Bagoas does. Alexander com</span>
  <span id="freeText18427457235308105852" style="display:none">Part of me really disliked this book; part of me couldn't help but like it. The language gets a little flowery sometimes, esp when she starts talking about love and (always cleverly elided) sex. The close adherence to history made parts of it read less-than-exciting. (i.e. "Great, now Alexander is going to preside over more games.... when is he going to drop dead alreadygjkfgjkdfg?!") I like Alexander but clearly not as much as the deadly combination of Renault-through-Bagoas does. Alexander comes across as vaguely obnoxious through the Bagoas filter; his actions sometimes seem reprehensible to me, but the text itself never acknowledges that. I may simply be nursing a grudge for Hephaestion's sake. Most incomprehensible of all to me was the strange falling-apart and yet not-falling-apart of Alexander and Hephaestion's relationship. Maybe I am too naturally jealous a person to understand why this fictional Hephaestion did not initially object to Alexander's taking on another lover. Bagoas' POV is too narrow and too biased, maybe; too much is unseen. Or maybe it just doesn't make sense.</span>

<div id="review_202423656" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202423656" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3713487-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314135581p2/3713487.jpg" /></a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who wants to know more about Alexander and his loves</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jane by: </span>
      booklist on Amazon
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202423656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9658095594104361118">An excellent well-written story of how an ex-prince became a eunuch and fulfilled his destiny in the arms of Alexander the Great!<br/><br/>His early years as a boy and his training as a court eunuch seemed to prepare him for his greatest love ....a love he did not think he would find.<br/><br/>He was Alexander's eromenus for the last eight years of Alexander's life. As great and devoted as he was he was jealous of Hephaestion's special love and place in Alexander's life. In fact it was reported that a Queen mistake</span>
  <span id="freeText9658095594104361118" style="display:none">An excellent well-written story of how an ex-prince became a eunuch and fulfilled his destiny in the arms of Alexander the Great!<br /><br />His early years as a boy and his training as a court eunuch seemed to prepare him for his greatest love ....a love he did not think he would find.<br /><br />He was Alexander's eromenus for the last eight years of Alexander's life. As great and devoted as he was he was jealous of Hephaestion's special love and place in Alexander's life. In fact it was reported that a Queen mistakenly bowed down to Hephaestion - mistaking him for Alexander! When she apologized Alexander's words were, "He too is Alexander, Madam." <br /><br />Bagoa, the eunich, at one point wanted to murder Hephaestion...but he learned that Alexander had love to give and loved giving it. <br /><br />However, when Hephaestion died, Alexander's grief was so great that Bagoas wished it was HE that had died in Hephaestion's place.<br /><br />But the author gives the reader so much more. ...a window into Alexander's daily life, his battles, his dealings with enemies.......he was fair beyond reproach.<br /><br />He was truly a living legend.......as I came to learn. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9658095594104361118" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vVfY7NjB6sqeSDL9nXyaBsuZ38heBCnpyVZ1NctxmkTkxDP23WuO1aFBBG3P69hjN2dIfIdfWjlvaK1amgQ9dA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10429545660485309401">Mary Renault was an English writer best known for her historical novels set in Ancient Greece. In addition to vivid fictional portrayals of Theseus, Socrates, Plato and Alexander the Great, she wrote a non-fiction biography of Alexander.<br/><br/>Her historical novels are all set in ancient Greece. They include a pair of novels about the mythological hero Theseus and a trilogy about the career of Alexander </span>
  <span id="freeText10429545660485309401" style="display:none">Mary Renault was an English writer best known for her historical novels set in Ancient Greece. In addition to vivid fictional portrayals of Theseus, Socrates, Plato and Alexander the Great, she wrote a non-fiction biography of Alexander.<br /><br />Her historical novels are all set in ancient Greece. They include a pair of novels about the mythological hero Theseus and a trilogy about the career of Alexander the Great. In a sense, The Charioteer (1953), the story of two young gay servicemen in the 1940s who try to model their relationship on the ideals expressed in Plato's Phaedrus and Symposium, is a warm-up for Renault's historical novels. By turning away from the 20th century and focusing on stories about male lovers in the warrior societies of ancient Greece, Renault no longer had to deal with homosexuality and anti-gay prejudice as social "problems". Instead she was free to focus on larger ethical and philosophical concerns, while examining the nature of love and leadership. The Charioteer could not be published in the U.S. until 1959, after the success of The Last of the Wine proved that American readers and critics would accept a serious gay love story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10429545660485309401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
